            <span title="Edit this template">
             e
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </div>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      <b>
       Paleontology
      </b>
      or
      <b>
       palaeontology
      </b>
      (
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="IPA nopopups">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">
         /
         <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
          <span title="// secondary stress follows">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'p' in 'pie'">
           p
          </span>
          <span title="/e/ long 'a' in 'base'">
           e
          </span>
          <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
           l
          </span>
          <span title="// short 'i' in 'bid'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="// short 'o' in 'body'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'n' in 'no'">
           n
          </span>
          <span title="// primary stress follows">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
           t
          </span>
          <span title="// short 'o' in 'body'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
           l
          </span>
          <span title="// 'a' in 'about'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="/d/ 'j' in 'jam'">
           d
          </span>
          <span title="/i/ 'y' in 'happy'">
           i
          </span>
         </span>
         /
        </a>
       </span>
      </span>
      ,
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="IPA nopopups">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">
         /
         <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
          <span title="// secondary stress follows">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'p' in 'pie'">
           p
          </span>
          <span title="/e/ long 'a' in 'base'">
           e
          </span>
          <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
           l
          </span>
          <span title="// short 'i' in 'bid'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="// 'a' in 'about'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'n' in 'no'">
           n
          </span>
          <span title="// primary stress follows">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
           t
          </span>
          <span title="// short 'o' in 'body'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
           l
          </span>
          <span title="// 'a' in 'about'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="/d/ 'j' in 'jam'">
           d
          </span>
          <span title="/i/ 'y' in 'happy'">
           i
          </span>
         </span>
         /
        </a>
       </span>
      </span>
      or
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="IPA nopopups">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">
         /
         <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
          <span title="// secondary stress follows">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'p' in 'pie'">
           p
          </span>
          <span title="// short 'a' in 'bad'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
           l
          </span>
          <span title="// short 'i' in 'bid'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="// short 'o' in 'body'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'n' in 'no'">
           n
          </span>
          <span title="// primary stress follows">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
           t
          </span>
          <span title="// short 'o' in 'body'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
           l
          </span>
          <span title="// 'a' in 'about'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="/d/ 'j' in 'jam'">
           d
          </span>
          <span title="/i/ 'y' in 'happy'">
           i
          </span>
         </span>
         /
        </a>
       </span>
      </span>
      ,
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="IPA nopopups">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">
         /
         <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
          <span title="// secondary stress follows">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'p' in 'pie'">
           p
          </span>
          <span title="// short 'a' in 'bad'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
           l
          </span>
          <span title="// short 'i' in 'bid'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="// 'a' in 'about'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'n' in 'no'">
           n
          </span>
          <span title="// primary stress follows">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
           t
          </span>
          <span title="// short 'o' in 'body'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
           l
          </span>
          <span title="// 'a' in 'about'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="/d/ 'j' in 'jam'">
           d
          </span>
          <span title="/i/ 'y' in 'happy'">
           i
          </span>
         </span>
         /
        </a>
       </span>
      </span>
      ) is the scientific study of life existent prior to, and sometimes including, the start of the
      <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">
       Holocene
      </a>
      Epoch roughly 11,700 years
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Before_present" title="Before present">
       before present
      </a>
      . It includes the study of fossils to determine organisms'
      <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">
       evolution
      </a>
      and interactions with each other and their environments (their
      <a href="/wiki/Paleoecology" title="Paleoecology">
       paleoecology
      </a>
      ). Paleontological observations have been documented as far back as the 5thcentury BC. The science became established in the 18thcentury as a result of
      <a href="/wiki/Georges_Cuvier" title="Georges Cuvier">
       Georges Cuvier
      </a>
      's work on
      <a href="/wiki/Comparative_anatomy" title="Comparative anatomy">
       comparative anatomy
      </a>
      , and developed rapidly in the 19thcentury. The term itself originates from
      <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
       Greek
      </a>
      ,
      <i>
       palaios
      </i>
      , i.e. "old, ancient", ,
      <i>
       on
      </i>
      (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genitive" title="Genitive">
       gen.
      </a>
      <i>
       ontos
      </i>
      ), i.e. "being, creature" and ,
      <i>
       logos
      </i>
      , i.e. "speech, thought, study".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OnlineEtDict_1-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-OnlineEtDict-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Paleontology lies on the border between
      <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">
       biology
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">
       geology
      </a>
      , but differs from
      <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">
       archaeology
      </a>
      in that it excludes the study of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">
       morphologically modern humans
      </a>
      . It now uses techniques drawn from a wide range of sciences, including
      <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">
       biochemistry
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematic" title="Mathematic">
       mathematics
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">
       engineering
      </a>
      . Use of all these techniques has enabled paleontologists to discover much of the
      <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_life" title="Evolutionary history of life">
       evolutionary history of life
      </a>
      , almost all the way back to when
      <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
       Earth
      </a>
      became capable of supporting life, about
      <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=3,800">
        3,800
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">
        million years ago
       </a>
      </span>
      . As knowledge has increased, paleontology has developed specialized sub-divisions, some of which focus on different types of
      <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">
       fossil
      </a>
      organisms while others study
      <a href="/wiki/Paleoecology" title="Paleoecology">
       ecology
      </a>
      and environmental history, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Paleoclimatology" title="Paleoclimatology">
       ancient climates
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Body fossils and
      <a href="/wiki/Trace_fossil" title="Trace fossil">
       trace fossils
      </a>
      are the principal types of evidence about ancient life, and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geochemical" title="Geochemical">
       geochemical
      </a>
      evidence has helped to decipher the evolution of life before there were organisms large enough to leave body fossils. Estimating the dates of these remains is essential but difficult: sometimes adjacent rock layers allow
      <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">
       radiometric dating
      </a>
      , which provides
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolute_dates" title="Absolute dates">
       absolute dates
      </a>
      that are accurate to within 0.5%, but more often paleontologists have to rely on relative dating by solving the "
      <a href="/wiki/Jigsaw_puzzle" title="Jigsaw puzzle">
       jigsaw puzzles
      </a>
      " of
      <a href="/wiki/Biostratigraphy" title="Biostratigraphy">
       biostratigraphy
      </a>
      . Classifying ancient organisms is also difficult, as many do not fit well into the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linnean_taxonomy" title="Linnean taxonomy">
       Linnean taxonomy
      </a>
      that is commonly used for classifying living organisms, and paleontologists more often use
      <a href="/wiki/Cladistics" title="Cladistics">
       cladistics
      </a>
      to draw up evolutionary "family trees". The final quarter of the 20th century saw the development of
      <a href="/wiki/Molecular_phylogenetics" title="Molecular phylogenetics">
       molecular phylogenetics
      </a>
      , which investigates how closely organisms are related by measuring how similar the
      <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">
       DNA
      </a>
      is in their
      <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">
       genomes
      </a>
      . Molecular phylogenetics has also been used to estimate the dates when species diverged, but there is controversy about the reliability of the
      <a href="/wiki/Molecular_clock" title="Molecular clock">
       molecular clock
      </a>
      on which such estimates depend.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">
       Overview
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Joda_paleontologist.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="339" data-file-width="452" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Joda_paleontologist.jpg/220px-Joda_paleontologist.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Joda_paleontologist.jpg/330px-Joda_paleontologist.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Joda_paleontologist.jpg/440px-Joda_paleontologist.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Joda_paleontologist.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A palaeontologist at work at
        <a href="/wiki/John_Day_Fossil_Beds_National_Monument" title="John Day Fossil Beds National Monument">
         John Day Fossil Beds National Monument
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europasaurus_Praeparation.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Europasaurus_Praeparation.JPG/220px-Europasaurus_Praeparation.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Europasaurus_Praeparation.JPG/330px-Europasaurus_Praeparation.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Europasaurus_Praeparation.JPG/440px-Europasaurus_Praeparation.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europasaurus_Praeparation.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The preparation of the fossilized bones of
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Europasaurus" title="Europasaurus">
          Europasaurus holgeri
         </a>
        </i>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The simplest definition is "the study of ancient life".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CowenHistLifeEd3Pxi_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CowenHistLifeEd3Pxi-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Paleontology seeks information about several aspects of past organisms: "their identity and origin, their environment and evolution, and what they can tell us about the Earth's organic and inorganic past".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="A_historical_science">
       A historical science
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: A historical science">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Paleontology is one of the historical sciences, along with
      <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">
       archaeology
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">
       geology
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">
       astronomy
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">
       cosmology
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">
       philology
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">
       history
      </a>
      itself.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laudan1992WhatSpecialP58_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Laudan1992WhatSpecialP58-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This means that it aims to describe phenomena of the past and reconstruct their causes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cleland2002MethodologicalAndEpistemicDifferences_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cleland2002MethodologicalAndEpistemicDifferences-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hence it has three main elements: description of the phenomena; developing a general theory about the causes of various types of change; and applying those theories to specific facts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laudan1992WhatSpecialP58_4-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Laudan1992WhatSpecialP58-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      When trying to explain past phenomena, paleontologists and other historical scientists often construct a set of hypotheses about the causes and then look for a
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Smoking_gun" title="Smoking gun">
        smoking gun
       </a>
      </i>
      , a piece of evidence that indicates that one hypotheses is a better explanation than others. Sometimes the smoking gun is discovered by a fortunate accident during other research. For example, the discovery by
      <a href="/wiki/Luis_Walter_Alvarez" title="Luis Walter Alvarez">
       Luis Alvarez
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Walter_Alvarez" title="Walter Alvarez">
       Walter Alvarez
      </a>
      of an
      <a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">
       iridium
      </a>
      -rich layer at the
      <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">
       Cretaceous
      </a>
      
      <a href="/wiki/Tertiary" title="Tertiary">
       Tertiary
      </a>
      boundary made
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroid_impact" title="Asteroid impact">
       asteroid impact
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Volcanism" title="Volcanism">
       volcanism
      </a>
      the most favored explanations for the
      <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="CretaceousPaleogene extinction event">
       CretaceousPaleogene extinction event
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cleland2002MethodologicalAndEpistemicDifferences_5-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cleland2002MethodologicalAndEpistemicDifferences-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The other main type of science is experimental science, which is often said to work by conducting
      <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">
       experiments
      </a>
      to
      <i>
       disprove
      </i>
      hypotheses about the workings and causes of natural phenomena note that this approach cannot confirm a hypothesis is correct, since some later experiment may disprove it. However, when confronted with totally unexpected phenomena, such as the first evidence for invisible
      <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">
       radiation
      </a>
      , experimental scientists often use the same approach as historical scientists: construct a set of hypotheses about the causes and then look for a "smoking gun".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cleland2002MethodologicalAndEpistemicDifferences_5-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cleland2002MethodologicalAndEpistemicDifferences-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Related_sciences">
       Related sciences
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Related sciences">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Paleontology lies on the boundary between
      <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">
       biology
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">
       geology
      </a>
      since paleontology focuses on the record of past life but its main source of evidence is
      <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">
       fossils
      </a>
      , which are found in rocks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For historical reasons paleontology is part of the geology departments of many universities, because in the 19th century and early 20th century geology departments found paleontological evidence important for estimating the ages of rocks while biology departments showed little interest.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laudan1992WhatSpecialP57_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Laudan1992WhatSpecialP57-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Paleontology also has some overlap with
      <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">
       archaeology
      </a>
      , which primarily works with objects made by humans and with human remains, while paleontologists are interested in the characteristics and evolution of humans as organisms. When dealing with evidence about humans, archaeologists and paleontologists may work together for example paleontologists might identify animal or plant fossils around an
      <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_site" title="Archaeological site">
       archaeological site
      </a>
      , to discover what the people who lived there ate; or they might analyze the climate at the time when the site was inhabited by humans.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCMPfaqAnthro_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-UCMPfaqAnthro-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fossil_Tyranausaurus_Rex_at_the_Royal_Tyrell_Museum,_Alberta,_Canada.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1777" data-file-width="2666" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Fossil_Tyranausaurus_Rex_at_the_Royal_Tyrell_Museum%2C_Alberta%2C_Canada.jpg/220px-Fossil_Tyranausaurus_Rex_at_the_Royal_Tyrell_Museum%2C_Alberta%2C_Canada.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Fossil_Tyranausaurus_Rex_at_the_Royal_Tyrell_Museum%2C_Alberta%2C_Canada.jpg/330px-Fossil_Tyranausaurus_Rex_at_the_Royal_Tyrell_Museum%2C_Alberta%2C_Canada.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Fossil_Tyranausaurus_Rex_at_the_Royal_Tyrell_Museum%2C_Alberta%2C_Canada.jpg/440px-Fossil_Tyranausaurus_Rex_at_the_Royal_Tyrell_Museum%2C_Alberta%2C_Canada.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fossil_Tyranausaurus_Rex_at_the_Royal_Tyrell_Museum,_Alberta,_Canada.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Analyses using
        <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">
         engineering
        </a>
        techniques show that
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurus" title="Tyrannosaurus">
          Tyrannosaurus
         </a>
        </i>
        had a devastating bite, but raise doubts about how fast it could move.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In addition paleontology often uses techniques derived from other sciences, including biology,
      <a href="/wiki/Osteology" title="Osteology">
       osteology
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">
       ecology
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">
       chemistry
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">
       physics
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">
       mathematics
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CowenHistLifeEd3Pxi_2-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-CowenHistLifeEd3Pxi-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For example
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geochemical" title="Geochemical">
       geochemical
      </a>
      signatures from rocks may help to discover when life first arose on Earth,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrasierMcLoughlinEtAl2006FreshLook_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BrasierMcLoughlinEtAl2006FreshLook-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and analyses of
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">
       carbon
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Isotope_analysis" title="Isotope analysis">
       isotope ratios
      </a>
      may help to identify climate changes and even to explain major transitions such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="PermianTriassic extinction event">
       PermianTriassic extinction event
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Twitchett_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Twitchett-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A relatively recent discipline,
      <a href="/wiki/Molecular_phylogenetics" title="Molecular phylogenetics">
       molecular phylogenetics
      </a>
      , often helps by using comparisons of different modern organisms'
      <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">
       DNA
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">
       RNA
      </a>
      to re-construct evolutionary "family trees"; it has also been used to estimate the dates of important evolutionary developments, although this approach is controversial because of doubts about the reliability of the "
      <a href="/wiki/Molecular_clock" title="Molecular clock">
       molecular clock
      </a>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PetersonEtAl2005_11-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-PetersonEtAl2005-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Techniques developed in
      <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">
       engineering
      </a>
      have been used to analyse how ancient organisms might have worked, for example how fast
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurus" title="Tyrannosaurus">
        Tyrannosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      could move and how powerful its bite was.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HutchinsonGarcia2002TrexSlow_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-HutchinsonGarcia2002TrexSlow-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MM03_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MM03-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is relatively commonplace to study fossils using
      <a href="/wiki/X-ray_microtomography" title="X-ray microtomography">
       X-ray microtomography
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A combination of paleontology, biology, and archaeology,
      <a href="/wiki/Paleoneurology" title="Paleoneurology">
       paleoneurology
      </a>
      is the study of endocranial casts (or endocasts) of species related to humans to learn about the evolution of human brains.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruner_15-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bruner-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Paleontology even contributes to
      <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">
       astrobiology
      </a>
      , the investigation of possible life on other
      <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">
       planets
      </a>
      , by developing models of how life may have arisen and by providing techniques for detecting evidence of life.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Subdivisions">
       Subdivisions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Subdivisions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      As knowledge has increased, paleontology has developed specialised subdivisions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate_paleontology" title="Vertebrate paleontology">
       Vertebrate paleontology
      </a>
      concentrates on
      <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">
       fossils
      </a>
      of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrates" title="Vertebrates">
       vertebrates
      </a>
      , from the earliest
      <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">
       fish
      </a>
      to the immediate ancestors of modern
      <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">
       mammals
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate_paleontology" title="Invertebrate paleontology">
       Invertebrate paleontology
      </a>
      deals with fossils of
      <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">
       invertebrates
      </a>
      such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusc" title="Mollusc">
       molluscs
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">
       arthropods
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Annelid" title="Annelid">
       annelid
      </a>
      worms and
      <a href="/wiki/Echinoderm" title="Echinoderm">
       echinoderms
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Paleobotany" title="Paleobotany">
       Paleobotany
      </a>
      focuses on the study of fossil
      <a href="/wiki/Embryophyte" title="Embryophyte">
       plants
      </a>
      , but traditionally includes the study of fossil
      <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">
       algae
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">
       fungi
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Palynology" title="Palynology">
       Palynology
      </a>
      , the study of
      <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">
       pollen
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spores" title="Spores">
       spores
      </a>
      produced by land plants and
      <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">
       protists
      </a>
      , straddles the border between paleontology and
      <a href="/wiki/Botany" title="Botany">
       botany
      </a>
      , as it deals with both living and fossil organisms.
      <a href="/wiki/Micropaleontology" title="Micropaleontology">
       Micropaleontology
      </a>
      deals with all microscopic fossil organisms, regardless of the group to which they belong.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCMPpaleoSpecialisms_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-UCMPpaleoSpecialisms-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Instead of focusing on individual organisms,
      <a href="/wiki/Paleoecology" title="Paleoecology">
       paleoecology
      </a>
      examines the interactions between different organisms, such as their places in
      <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">
       food chains
      </a>
      , and the two-way interaction between organisms and their environment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      One example is the development of
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen#Photosynthesis_and_respiration" title="Oxygen">
       oxygenic photosynthesis
      </a>
      by
      <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">
       bacteria
      </a>
      , which hugely increased the productivity and diversity of
      <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">
       ecosystems
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This also caused the
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen#Build-up_in_the_atmosphere" title="Oxygen">
       oxygenation of the atmosphere
      </a>
      . Together, these were a prerequisite for the evolution of the most complex
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eucaryotic" title="Eucaryotic">
       eucaryotic
      </a>
      cells, from which all
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multicellular" title="Multicellular">
       multicellular
      </a>
      organisms are built.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HedgesBlairEtAl2004molecularTimescaleOfEukaryoteEvolution_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-HedgesBlairEtAl2004molecularTimescaleOfEukaryoteEvolution-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Paleoclimatology" title="Paleoclimatology">
       Paleoclimatology
      </a>
      , although sometimes treated as part of paleoecology,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCMPpaleoSpecialisms_18-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-UCMPpaleoSpecialisms-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      focuses more on the history of Earth's climate and the mechanisms that have changed it
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
       which have sometimes included
      <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">
       evolutionary
      </a>
      developments, for example the rapid expansion of land plants in the
      <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">
       Devonian
      </a>
      period removed more
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
       carbon dioxide
      </a>
      from the atmosphere, reducing the
      <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">
       greenhouse effect
      </a>
      and thus helping to cause an
      <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">
       ice age
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">
       Carboniferous
      </a>
      period.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlgeoScheckler1998errestrialMarineTeleconnectionsInDevonian_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AlgeoScheckler1998errestrialMarineTeleconnectionsInDevonian-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Biostratigraphy" title="Biostratigraphy">
       Biostratigraphy
      </a>
      , the use of fossils to work out the chronological order in which rocks were formed, is useful to both paleontologists and geologists.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Biogeography" title="Biogeography">
       Biogeography
      </a>
      studies the spatial distribution of organisms, and is also linked to geology, which explains how Earth's geography has changed over time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sources_of_evidence">
       Sources of evidence
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Sources of evidence">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Body_fossils">
       Body fossils
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Body fossils">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossils" title="Fossils">
       Fossils
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marrella_(fossil).png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="401" data-file-width="516" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Marrella_%28fossil%29.png/220px-Marrella_%28fossil%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Marrella_%28fossil%29.png/330px-Marrella_%28fossil%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Marrella_%28fossil%29.png/440px-Marrella_%28fossil%29.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marrella_(fossil).png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        This
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Marrella" title="Marrella">
          Marrella
         </a>
        </i>
        specimen illustrates how clear and detailed the fossils from the
        <a href="/wiki/Burgess_Shale" title="Burgess Shale">
         Burgess Shale
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Lagerst%C3%A4tte" title="Lagersttte">
         lagersttte
        </a>
        are.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Fossils of organisms' bodies are usually the most informative type of evidence. The most common types are wood, bones, and shells.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCMPWhatIsPaleo_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-UCMPWhatIsPaleo-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Fossilisation is a rare event, and most fossils are destroyed by
      <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">
       erosion
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Metamorphism" title="Metamorphism">
       metamorphism
      </a>
      before they can be observed. Hence the fossil record is very incomplete, increasingly so further back in time. Despite this, it is often adequate to illustrate the broader patterns of life's history.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BentonQualityFossilRecord_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BentonQualityFossilRecord-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There are also biases in the fossil record: different environments are more favorable to the preservation of different types of organism or parts of organisms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butterfield2003ExceptionalFossilPreservation_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Butterfield2003ExceptionalFossilPreservation-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Further, only the parts of organisms that were already
      <a href="/wiki/Mineralization_(biology)" title="Mineralization (biology)">
       mineralised
      </a>
      are usually preserved, such as the shells of molluscs. Since most animal species are soft-bodied, they decay before they can become fossilised. As a result, although there are 30-plus
      <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">
       phyla
      </a>
      of living animals, two-thirds have never been found as fossils.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CowenHistLifeEd3P61_29-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CowenHistLifeEd3P61-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Occasionally, unusual environments may preserve soft tissues. These
      <a href="/wiki/Lagerst%C3%A4tte" title="Lagersttte">
       lagersttten
      </a>
      allow paleontologists to examine the internal anatomy of animals that in other sediments are represented only by shells, spines, claws, etc. if they are preserved at all. However, even lagersttten present an incomplete picture of life at the time. The majority of organisms living at the time are probably not represented because lagersttten are restricted to a narrow range of environments, e.g. where soft-bodied organisms can be preserved very quickly by events such as mudslides; and the exceptional events that cause quick burial make it difficult to study the normal environments of the animals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The sparseness of the fossil record means that organisms are expected to exist long before and after they are found in the fossil record this is known as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signor-Lipps_effect" title="Signor-Lipps effect">
       Signor-Lipps effect
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Signor1982_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Signor1982-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Trace_fossils">
       Trace fossils
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Trace fossils">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CambrianRusophycus.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/CambrianRusophycus.jpg/220px-CambrianRusophycus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/CambrianRusophycus.jpg/330px-CambrianRusophycus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/CambrianRusophycus.jpg/440px-CambrianRusophycus.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CambrianRusophycus.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">
         Cambrian
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Trace_fossil" title="Trace fossil">
         trace fossils
        </a>
        including
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Rusophycus" title="Rusophycus">
          Rusophycus
         </a>
        </i>
        , made by a
        <a href="/wiki/Trilobite" title="Trilobite">
         trilobite
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Trace_fossil" title="Trace fossil">
       Trace fossil
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trace_fossils" title="Trace fossils">
       Trace fossils
      </a>
      consist mainly of tracks and burrows, but also include
      <a href="/wiki/Coprolite" title="Coprolite">
       coprolites
      </a>
      (fossil
      <a href="/wiki/Feces" title="Feces">
       feces
      </a>
      ) and marks left by feeding.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCMPWhatIsPaleo_26-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-UCMPWhatIsPaleo-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FedonkinGehlingEtAl2007RiseOfAnimals_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FedonkinGehlingEtAl2007RiseOfAnimals-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Trace fossils are particularly significant because they represent a data source that is not limited to animals with easily fossilized hard parts, and they reflect organisms' behaviours. Also many traces date from significantly earlier than the body fossils of animals that are thought to have been capable of making them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seilacher1994_33-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Seilacher1994-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Whilst exact assignment of trace fossils to their makers is generally impossible, traces may for example provide the earliest physical evidence of the appearance of moderately complex animals (comparable to
      <a href="/wiki/Earthworm" title="Earthworm">
       earthworms
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FedonkinGehlingEtAl2007RiseOfAnimals_32-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-FedonkinGehlingEtAl2007RiseOfAnimals-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Geochemical_observations">
       Geochemical observations
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Geochemical observations">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Geochemistry" title="Geochemistry">
       Geochemistry
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Geochemical observations may help to deduce the global level of biological activity, or the affinity of certain fossils. For example geochemical features of rocks may reveal when life first arose on Earth,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrasierMcLoughlinEtAl2006FreshLook_9-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-BrasierMcLoughlinEtAl2006FreshLook-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and may provide evidence of the presence of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eucaryotic" title="Eucaryotic">
       eucaryotic
      </a>
      cells, the type from which all
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multicellular" title="Multicellular">
       multicellular
      </a>
      organisms are built.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrocksLoganEtAl1999RiseOfEukaryotes_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BrocksLoganEtAl1999RiseOfEukaryotes-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Analyses of
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">
       carbon
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Isotope_analysis" title="Isotope analysis">
       isotope ratios
      </a>
      may help to explain major transitions such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="PermianTriassic extinction event">
       PermianTriassic extinction event
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Twitchett_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Twitchett-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Classifying_ancient_organisms">
       Classifying ancient organisms
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Classifying ancient organisms">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_classification" title="Biological classification">
       Biological classification
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cladistics" title="Cladistics">
       Cladistics
      </a>

      <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_nomenclature" title="Phylogenetic nomenclature">
       Phylogenetic nomenclature
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_taxonomy" title="Evolutionary taxonomy">
       Evolutionary taxonomy
      </a>
     </div>

                        </b>

                       <tr>
                        <td style="width: 1.5em; border-bottom: 1px solid black; border-left: 0; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">

                         </p>

                        <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 0; vertical-align: top;">

                        <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 1px solid black; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">

                          </a>

                        </td>
                       </tr>
                      </table>
                     </td>
                    </tr>
                    <tr>
                     <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">

                  </td>
                 </tr>

                  <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
                   <br/>
                  </td>
                 </tr>
                </table>
               </td>
              </tr>
              <tr>
               <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
                <br/>
               </td>
              </tr>
             </table>
            </td>
           </tr>
           <tr>
            <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
             <br/>
            </td>
           </tr>
          </table>
         </td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
         <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
          <br/>
         </td>
        </tr>
       </table>
      </div>
      <b>
       Simple example cladogram
      </b>
      <br/>
      <span style="background:red;">
      </span>
      Warm-bloodedness evolved somewhere in the
      <br/>
      synapsidmammal transition.
      <br/>
      <span style="background:red; color:yellow;">
       <b>
        ?
       </b>
      </span>
      Warm-bloodedness must also have evolved at one of
      <br/>
      these points an example of
      <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">
       convergent evolution
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CowenHistLifeEd3P47To50_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CowenHistLifeEd3P47To50-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Biological_classification_L_Pengo.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="590" data-file-width="230" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Biological_classification_L_Pengo.svg/100px-Biological_classification_L_Pengo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Biological_classification_L_Pengo.svg/150px-Biological_classification_L_Pengo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Biological_classification_L_Pengo.svg/200px-Biological_classification_L_Pengo.svg.png 2x" width="100"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Biological_classification_L_Pengo.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Levels in the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linnean_taxonomy" title="Linnean taxonomy">
         Linnean taxonomy
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Naming groups of organisms in a way that is clear and widely agreed is important, as some disputes in paleontology have been based just on misunderstandings over names.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrochuSumrall2001_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BrochuSumrall2001-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linnean_taxonomy" title="Linnean taxonomy">
       Linnean taxonomy
      </a>
      is commonly used for classifying living organisms, but runs into difficulties when dealing with newly discovered organisms that are significantly different from known ones. For example: it is hard to decide at what level to place a new higher-level grouping, e.g.
      <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">
       genus
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">
       family
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">
       order
      </a>
      ; this is important since the Linnean rules for naming groups are tied to their levels, and hence if a group is moved to a different level it must be renamed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Paleontologists generally use approaches based on
      <a href="/wiki/Cladistics" title="Cladistics">
       cladistics
      </a>
      , a technique for working out the evolutionary "family tree" of a set of organisms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrochuSumrall2001_36-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-BrochuSumrall2001-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It works by the logic that, if groups B and C have more similarities to each other than either has to group A, then B and C are more closely related to each other than either is to A. Characters that are compared may be
      <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">
       anatomical
      </a>
      , such as the presence of a
      <a href="/wiki/Notochord" title="Notochord">
       notochord
      </a>
      , or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_phylogeny" title="Molecular phylogeny">
       molecular
      </a>
      , by comparing sequences of
      <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">
       DNA
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">
       proteins
      </a>
      . The result of a successful analysis is a hierarchy of clades groups that share a common ancestor. Ideally the "family tree" has only two branches leading from each node ("junction"), but sometimes there is too little information to achieve this and paleontologists have to make do with junctions that have several branches. The cladistic technique is sometimes fallible, as some features, such as wings or
      <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_the_eye" title="Evolution of the eye">
       camera eyes
      </a>
      , evolved more than once,
      <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">
       convergently
      </a>
       this must be taken into account in analyses.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CowenHistLifeEd3P47To50_35-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-CowenHistLifeEd3P47To50-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_developmental_biology" title="Evolutionary developmental biology">
       Evolutionary developmental biology
      </a>
      , commonly abbreviated to "Evo Devo", also helps paleontologists to produce "family trees". For example the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embryological" title="Embryological">
       embryological
      </a>
      development of some modern
      <a href="/wiki/Brachiopod" title="Brachiopod">
       brachiopods
      </a>
      suggests that brachiopods may be descendants of the
      <a href="/wiki/Halkieriid" title="Halkieriid">
       halkieriids
      </a>
      , which became extinct in the
      <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">
       Cambrian
      </a>
      period.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Estimating_the_dates_of_organisms">
       Estimating the dates of organisms
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Estimating the dates of organisms">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Geochronology" title="Geochronology">
       Geochronology
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb" style="float:right; clear:right; margin-left:5px; border:solid #ccc 1px; padding:2px; width:340px;;">
      <div class="thumbinner image" style="position:relative; width:332px; height:{{{height}}}px; border:solid #ccc 1px; background-color:white;">
       <div style=";left:0px; top:0px; width:338px; position:absolute;">

       <div style="visibility:hidden">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Index_fossils_blank_01.png">
         <img alt="" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="675" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Index_fossils_blank_01.png/338px-Index_fossils_blank_01.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Index_fossils_blank_01.png/507px-Index_fossils_blank_01.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3f/Index_fossils_blank_01.png 2x" width="338"/>
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumbcaption" style="width:332px;">
       Common
       <a href="/wiki/Index_fossil" title="Index fossil">
        index fossils
       </a>
       used to date rocks in North-East USA
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Paleontology seeks to map out how living things have changed through time. A substantial hurdle to this aim is the difficulty of working out how old fossils are. Beds that preserve fossils typically lack the radioactive elements needed for
      <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">
       radiometric dating
      </a>
      . This technique is our only means of giving rocks greater than about 50 million years old an absolute age, and can be accurate to within 0.5% or better.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin2000_39-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Martin2000-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although radiometric dating requires very careful laboratory work, its basic principle is simple: the rates at which various radioactive elements
      <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">
       decay
      </a>
      are known, and so the ratio of the radioactive element to the element into which it decays shows how long ago the radioactive element was incorporated into the rock. Radioactive elements are common only in rocks with a volcanic origin, and so the only fossil-bearing rocks that can be dated radiometrically are a few volcanic ash layers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin2000_39-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Martin2000-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Consequently, paleontologists must usually rely on
      <a href="/wiki/Stratigraphy" title="Stratigraphy">
       stratigraphy
      </a>
      to date fossils. Stratigraphy is the science of deciphering the "layer-cake" that is the
      <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">
       sedimentary
      </a>
      record, and has been compared to a
      <a href="/wiki/Jigsaw_puzzle" title="Jigsaw puzzle">
       jigsaw puzzle
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Rocks normally form relatively horizontal layers, with each layer younger than the one underneath it. If a fossil is found between two layers whose ages are known, the fossil's age must lie between the two known ages.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Because rock sequences are not continuous, but may be broken up by
      <a href="/wiki/Fault_(geology)" title="Fault (geology)">
       faults
      </a>
      or periods of
      <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">
       erosion
      </a>
      , it is very difficult to match up rock beds that are not directly next to one another. However, fossils of species that survived for a relatively short time can be used to link up isolated rocks: this technique is called
      <i>
       biostratigraphy
      </i>
      . For instance, the conodont
      <i>
       Eoplacognathus pseudoplanus
      </i>
      has a short range in the Middle Ordovician period.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      If rocks of unknown age are found to have traces of
      <i>
       E. pseudoplanus
      </i>
      , they must have a mid-Ordovician age. Such
      <a href="/wiki/Index_fossil" title="Index fossil">
       index fossils
      </a>
      must be distinctive, be globally distributed and have a short time range to be useful. However, misleading results are produced if the index fossils turn out to have longer fossil ranges than first thought.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Stratigraphy and biostratigraphy can in general provide only relative dating (
      <i>
       A
      </i>
      was before
      <i>
       B
      </i>
      ), which is often sufficient for studying evolution. However, this is difficult for some time periods, because of the problems involved in matching up rocks of the same age across different
      <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">
       continents
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gehling2001_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gehling2001-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Family-tree relationships may also help to narrow down the date when lineages first appeared. For instance, if fossils of B or C date to Xmillion years ago and the calculated "family tree" says A was an ancestor of B and C, then A must have evolved more than Xmillion years ago.
     </p>
     <p>
      It is also possible to estimate how long ago two living clades diverged i.e. approximately how long ago their last common ancestor must have lived  by assuming that DNA
      <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">
       mutations
      </a>
      accumulate at a constant rate. These "
      <a href="/wiki/Molecular_clock" title="Molecular clock">
       molecular clocks
      </a>
      ", however, are fallible, and provide only a very approximate timing: for example, they are not sufficiently precise and reliable for estimating when the groups that feature in the
      <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_explosion" title="Cambrian explosion">
       Cambrian explosion
      </a>
      first evolved,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and estimates produced by different techniques may vary by a factor of two.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PetersonEtAl2005_11-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-PetersonEtAl2005-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>

       Overview of the history of life
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Overview of the history of life">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>

      <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_life" title="Evolutionary history of life">
       Evolutionary history of life
